Theodore Roosevelt, An Autobiography by Theodore Roosevelt

Chapter 16, The Men in the Arena

By the time I had met Mr. Taft, it had been a few years after that fateful 1912 campaign. Bitter I was over the razor-thin defeat, I had retreated to Oyster Bay and had largely sat out of the campaign. Many of my advisors had confided in me that there was a real argument for me to claim that there were errors in the awarding of delegates in the south, and that it could have secured me the nomination. Despite this, Taft was still able to defeat `+opName+`, and I did indeed vote for him.

The meeting was at a hotel in Chicago. It was not an intentional one, and in fact, it was Mr. Taft who had made my contact again, to my surprise. We had spent several minutes discussing the politics of the day - Taft was on his way out as President, I was still considering whether to allow myself to be drafted in 1916 the same way I had been drafted in 1912. The conversation was both about politics and of personal life - Taft asked about the convention, and my family. By the end, it had felt like Taft was not just a politician - he had become an elder statesmen.

My regrets about the 1912 campaign still linger, and for reasons I cannot explain, they lingered more after this conversation. Many asked why I did not contest the 1920 campaign, one that many have written to tell me that I could very well in. In many ways, I believe it was this conversation that had convinced me not to. Taft had shown me the way I could lead despite not being in the arena - part of me had wondered if I should have considered this in 1912.

Absolute History | YouTube

1. When Grover Cleveland was drafted to fight in the American Civil War, he simply paid someone else $150 to go and fight for him - which was... apparently something he could do back then.

2. Benjamin Harrison wasn't particularly interesting but was the last president to have a beard. Till we meet again, old friend.

4. William McKinley claimed that he didn't want to see the US in another conflict following his time in the Civil War. Despite this he oversaw a dramatic increase in American militarism, including the Spanish-American War

5. Theodore Roosevelt, despite his tough and energetic personality, grew up with poor health, and frequently had to deal with asthma attack. In fact, it was likely good exercise and a desire for adventure that helped improve his health.

6. William Howard Taft loved to golf. From the onset of his Presidency, Theodore Roosevelt warned him that it was considered a "rich man's game", yet he persisted in playing the game. So much in fact, that when he ran for re-election, he essentially just played golf rather than actually campaign.

The Election of 1928

Holt McDougal's United States History Textbooks | Chapter 14

As discussed in the previous chapter, Taft's decision to lower tariffs had an outsized effect on the political landscape. When 1928 rolled around, both parties quickly caught onto the new base of support they had recieved. Republicans had managed to begin making real inroads into the south, both with their tariff policy and through nominating national hero and Lily-White Herbert Hoover. Meanwhile, New York protectionist Royal Copeland championed the Democratic nomination after convincing the convention to pass a mildly pro-tariff platform.

Despite the unpopularity of the Wood administration, Hoover kept the race competitive through his national name recognition and appeal to the south. Ultimately, though, it was Mr. Copeland's ability to appeal to protectionists up north and his ability to keep enough of the solid south into the fold in order to secure a 274-257 majority in the Electoral College [Pictured to the left].

Of course, Copeland would only go on to serve one term. His willingness to raise tariffs meant that when the Wall Street crash of April 1929 began, he was pressured by Congress to drastically raise rates, setting the stage for the Great Depression.

`) //e.image = "" e.executable.push([2, (() => {e.image = e.image = 'https://itsastronomical.com/assets/1912/endings/schizomap.png'})]) } else if (trade >= 1) { e.pages.push(`

UNIONIST LIBERALS SWEEP CANADIAN ELECTIONS, PRIME MINISTER LAURIER LOSES QUEBEC EAST RIDING

December 10th, 1916 | The Chicago Tribune

OTTAWA - The Liberal Party of Canada shall continue their twenty year tenure in Government, as voters have returned a strong majority government for the Unionist Liberals in the midst of the Great War. Such a victory, however, came at the cost of Prime Minister Wilfrid Laurier losing his own riding in Quebec East to a candidate from the anti-conscription, pro-French Action Nationale Party.

While the Unionist Liberals made massive gains in the Anglophone provinces such as Ontario, British Columbia, and Manitoba, the traditionally Liberal province of Quebec saw large swings against the party, namely for their opposition to involvement in the Great War. Many of these former Liberal votes went to the Action Nationale Party, which has picked up 39 of the 65 seats in Quebec - with 15 being retained by the Liberals, 2 by the Conservatives, and 9 seats being undeclared as of now. It is expected that, following this loss, Laurier shall either seek election in a by-election, or he shall retire after a 20-year tenure as Prime Minister and almost 30 years as the Liberal Party leader.

The New York Times | June 29th, 1914

Sarajevo, Bosnia, June 28. (By courtesy of the Vienna Neue Freie Presse.)--Archduke Francis Ferdinand, heir to the throne of Austria-Hungary, and his wife, the Duchess of Hohenberg, were shot and killed by a Bosnian student here today. The fatal shooting was the second attempt upon the lives of the couple during the day, and is believed to have been the result of a political conspiracy.

This morning, as Archduke Francis Ferdinand and the Duchess were driving to a reception at the Town Hall a bomb was thrown at their motor car. The Archduke pushed it off with his arm.

dragHeader.onmousedown = function (e) { isDragging = true; offsetX = e.clientX - panel.getBoundingClientRect().left; offsetY = e.clientY - panel.getBoundingClientRect().top; document.body.style.userSelect = 'none'; }; document.onmousemove = function (e) { if (isDragging) { panel.style.left = (e.clientX - offsetX) + 'px'; panel.style.top = (e.clientY - offsetY) + 'px'; } }; document.onmouseup = function () { isDragging = false; document.body.style.userSelect = ''; }; } function makeDraggable(element) { let isDragging = false; let offsetX, offsetY; element.onmousedown = function(event) { isDragging = true; offsetX = event.clientX - element.getBoundingClientRect().left; offsetY = event.clientY - element.getBoundingClientRect().top; document.onmousemove = function(event) { if (isDragging) { const newX = event.clientX - offsetX; const newY = event.clientY - offsetY; // Update the position of the element element.style.left = newX + 'px'; element.style.top = newY + 'px'; // Save the position in global variables savedX = newX; savedY = newY; } }; document.onmouseup = function() { isDragging = false; document.onmousemove = null; document.onmouseup = null; }; }; } function refreshSurrogates() { const hq_tracker_panel = document.getElementById('hq_tracker_panel'); if (hq_tracker_panel) { hq_tracker_panel.remove(); openHQTracker(); } } // refreshSurrogates(); const windowCheck = localStorage.getItem('astroWindow'); function handleMutations(mutationsList, observer) { if (observerRunning) return; observerRunning = true; if (windowCheck == "true"){ addHQTrackerButton(); } else { addHeadquarterButton(); } observer.observe(document.documentElement, { childList: true, subtree: true }); observerRunning = false; } let observerRunning = false; var element = document.getElementById('controlElement'); if (!element) { let singleObserver = new MutationObserver(handleMutations); singleObserver.observe(document.documentElement, { childList: true, subtree: true }); var controlElement = document.createElement('div'); controlElement.style.display = 'none'; controlElement.id = 'controlElement'; document.body.appendChild(controlElement); }
